https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=415 415w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=480 480w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=540 540w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=640 640w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=750 750w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=828 828w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1080 1080w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1200 1200w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1440 1440w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1920 1920w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2048 2048w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400 2400w" src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22415070/spot1_v3.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400"/></a></div></div></div><div class="duet--media--caption xptnl10"><cite class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl11">Illustration by <a href="https://carmellekendall.com/">Carmelle Kendall</a></cite></div></div></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">In Missouri, Danielle has worked as a senior development teacher at a center that doubled as a university learning laboratory for four years, and has a degree in ECE with a focus on teaching young children. Her salary is currently just over $28,000, and even though she splits rent for a small home with her partner who works at a nonprofit, they still struggle to cover costs from month to month and live paycheck to paycheck. Her student loans have been in forbearance since she graduated from college in 2019. She recently watched a coworker leave her position to work at a bank, where she now makes double her old salary. Other coworkers regularly leave for fast food jobs.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">At a previous center, the director would often tell workers to be mindful of just how lucky they were, despite their low pay, to have health insurance and paid leave. &ldquo;But these are basic needs of employees,&rdquo; Danielle tells me. &ldquo;Health insurance is a need, not a want. Paid leave is necessary for teachers to still maintain their own lives outside of work.&rdquo;</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">&ldquo;It makes me feel so underappreciated that teachers working in K-8 are taken more seriously and paid way more than ECE educators,&rdquo; she continues. &ldquo;But there is a vast difference in how our society views K-8 teachers compared to ECE.&rdquo;</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">All over the country, there are people who feel called to the work, who want to continue doing the work, but simply cannot afford to keep doing the work. Because in the end, none of the piecemeal solutions actually address the real problem: that instead of figuring out ways to make care into a public good and caregiving into a good job, many people still think that women, preferably children&rsquo;s own mothers, should be doing this work for free.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">&ldquo;Here in Charleston, there are many [ECE] jobs that start at $9 and $10 an hour,&rdquo; Danielle explains. &ldquo;The profession seems to be looked at as a babysitter, something that a housewife can do for some extra cash,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But that&rsquo;s not how we provide quality education and care, and that&rsquo;s not a living wage.&rdquo;</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf1 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><hr class="duet--article--divider _1agbrixj w7vpv0"/></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Over the past 50 years, the decline of the American middle class has been inseparable from the decline in unionized manufacturing jobs, which paid well enough for many families to be supported by a single salary. As those jobs disappeared, more and more middle-class women entered the workforce, spiking the demand for child care outside the home. The two shifts cannot be untangled: As one sector declined, another rose, at least in part, in response. Both were performed by a mix of workers with and without college degrees, but for many reasons, including a burst of anti-union sentiment and a resistance to viewing child care as a skilled profession, the child care workforce did not unionize en masse. Even as the workforce became more and more essential, the jobs never even came close to providing the sort of pay and benefits that made a manufacturing job a potential pathway to the middle class.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Those manufacturing jobs aren&rsquo;t coming back, at least not in any approximation of what they once were.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Instead of waxing about their decline, we could shift our attention to transforming a whole sector of existing jobs, jobs with incredibly high demand, jobs that serve as the backbone of our personal and public lives, into good, middle-class jobs. This transformation could have cascading effects on the lives of the existing, majority-white middle class, many of whose middle-class salaries depend on the ability to access care. The greatest economic effect, however, would be to open up the middle class to over 2 million caregivers &mdash; and remember that <a href="https://cscce.berkeley.edu/racial-wage-gaps-in-early-education-employment/">40 percent</a> are women of color.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">UC Berkeley&rsquo;s <a href="https://cscce.berkeley.edu/">Center for the Study of Child Care Employment</a> has calculated what it would cost to transform <a href="https://cscce.berkeley.edu/financing-early-educator-quality-a-values-based-budget-for-every-state/">each state&rsquo;s current system</a> into one that was &ldquo;values&rdquo; based, with a &ldquo;well-qualified and fairly compensated early care workforce&rdquo; providing high standards of care. In Missouri, where Danielle lives, the proposed increase in funding would raise salaries for early educators with bachelor&rsquo;s degrees to $49,626 (on par with elementary and middle school educators) and teacher&rsquo;s assistants to $29,776. Both positions would come with benefits including paid time off and health insurance.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">With this proposal, Danielle&rsquo;s current income would be doubled. &ldquo;It would change my life significantly,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;It could lead to me living a comfortable, less stressful life. I could pay off my debts, my student loans, and even be able to save for my future.&rdquo; Doubling pay might seem extreme, but these salary increases aren&rsquo;t exorbitant. What they are is stabilizing: for the millions of kids in care, for the millions of parents who depend on it, but also for the millions of women who do this work.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">&ldquo;The stimulus plan is an interesting opportunity to say, we&rsquo;re capable of making significant investments in people when everyone can see the need,&rdquo; Molly Sullivan, the director of national initiatives at First Children&rsquo;s Finance, says. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t often publicly talk about not being able to afford child care. It&rsquo;s often a private struggle that we keep out of the spotlight, and I think there&rsquo;s an opportunity to see this as a wake-up call. It&rsquo;s okay to acknowledge that what we were doing in the past wasn&rsquo;t working, so let&rsquo;s build a system that didn&rsquo;t exist.&rdquo;</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">That system will be complicated. You can read about <a href="https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2019-12-13/how-universal-pre-k-affects-infant-daycare-costs">what happens</a> when you extend universal pre-K, as New York, Boston, Nashville, San Antonio, Charlotte, and a handful of other cities have, and don&rsquo;t consider what sucking the older students out of the system does to drive up the price of toddler and infant care. You can deliberate whether the funding should come directly from the federal government or remain the provenance of the states, and how that will reinforce existing discrepancies in educational outcomes in places like Idaho and Mississippi that refuse federal dollars. Some people told me there&rsquo;s too much demand in some areas with universal pre-K, with the same long waiting lists as private preschool, and others directed me to complicated rules about when a pre-K student enters the system, which then make it difficult to give a kid an extra year, if they need it, before moving on to kindergarten.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">It&rsquo;s a tangled set of problems, set against a backdrop of historical and cultural prejudices &mdash; and an undeniable part of the current quagmire, as Anna North <a href="/22321909/covid-19-pandemic-school-work-parents-remote">recently argued</a>, is the sheer number of hours that Americans have to work to arrive at a living wage. But as Sullivan put it to me, &ldquo;as a country, we have proved that we can tackle big, complicated, incredibly complex things, from the WPA to the Covid vaccine rollout. This is just an area where policy has not really ventured yet.&rdquo; At least not in a substantive way that doesn&rsquo;t just fiddle with the margins &mdash; and at least not since 1971, when Nixon chose that other fork in the road, and mired us in our current, broken approach.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 lg8ac5a xkp0cg1">We&rsquo;ve shown that we can care about inequities even when they don&rsquo;t primarily affect us. We&rsquo;ve shown that we can support public projects even when we are not the primary beneficiaries. If you&rsquo;re drowning in child care costs right now, probably none of this will get done in time to help you. That&rsquo;s the thing about conceiving of child care as a public good: It will serve you, someday, in ways you might not yet appreciate. A public good is good, in some way, for everyone. Plug the hole in the bucket, and the benefits overflow.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1"><em>If you&rsquo;d like to share your experience as part of the hollow middle class with The Goods, email </em><a href="mailto:annehelenpetersen@vox.com"><em><strong>annehelenpetersen@vox.com</strong></em></a><em> or fill out </em><a href="https://docs.google.com/forms/d/e/1FAIpQLScc-Rm_NMsyRPfevs25lhxHFoq2k72fwxyhGpgEOpWbSxZjoQ/viewform?gxids=7628"><em><strong>this form</strong></em></a><em>.</em></p></div><div alt="The escalating costs of being single in America" data-chromatic="ignore" loading="lazy" decoding="async" data-nimg="fill" class="_1agz5xbg mvmjsc0" I graduated from college in the mid-2000s, I moved to Seattle to find a job waitressing. After weeks of handing over r\u0026eacute;sum\u0026eacute;s, I was still unemployed. Then I saw an advertisement in the local alt-weekly for a teacher\u0026rsquo;s assistant position at a preschool, just across the street from the University of Washington. I\u0026rsquo;d babysat, usually for around $2 an hour, since fifth grade. Infants, toddlers, 4-year-olds: I\u0026rsquo;d spent endless hours with them all, sometimes all at once, sometimes even overnight. I had great references. I got the job, which paid $8 an hour \u0026mdash; just over the state\u0026rsquo;s then-minimum wage of $7.16 \u0026mdash; almost instantly."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"In the toddler room, where kids were between 1 and 2 years old, another teacher and I handled the care of a dozen squawking, endlessly curious kids. The job, as anyone who\u0026rsquo;s worked in a child care center or preschool can tell you, is incredibly physical; I was sore every night in some new way."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"This was before the Affordable Care Act, and I had no health insurance. I caught colds and flus from the kids, got the familiar tingle of strep throat, but instead of getting antibiotics, I let it ride out, lucky it didn\u0026rsquo;t get worse. I was living with three friends in a small house nearby and watched as the $500-a-month rent, plus $80 a month for a subsidized bus pass, ate up the majority of my paycheck. I tried to cut costs by grazing on the leftovers of the food provided for the kids for breakfast and lunch. My small savings, accumulated over the summer while working at a dude ranch, began to dwindle."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"When a friend told me about a nanny agency that could get me at least $13 an hour, paid time off, and a stipend for health insurance, how could I say no? I could\u0026rsquo;ve taken some night classes to get my early childhood accreditation, but that would bump my pay by a dollar, maybe slightly more. I loved those kids, and I honestly loved the group care environment, which would stand in stark contrast to my long, lonely days as a nanny. But I couldn\u0026rsquo;t survive on the pay long term. So I became a statistic: one of hundreds of thousands of workers who leave early childhood care and education \u0026mdash; an umbrella term for home child care, child care centers, and private and public preschools for children under 5 \u0026mdash; every year for higher-paying work, whether at the Starbucks down the road or in the K-8 public school system."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"The vast majority of early childhood jobs, at least as they\u0026rsquo;re currently conceived, are not good jobs. The pay is breathtakingly low: In 2019, the median was \u003ca href=\"https://www.bls.gov/ooh/personal-care-and-service/childcare-workers.htm\"\u003e$11.65 an hour\u003c/a\u003e, or $24,230 a year. As a result, turnover is incredibly high. A recent study in Louisiana found that every year more than one-third of early childhood educators \u003ca href=\"https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.3102/0162373720985340\"\u003eleave their jobs\u003c/a\u003e. Not for competitors, not for slightly higher-paying jobs. They leave the sector altogether."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"Between 43 and 54 percent of early childhood workers are \u003ca href=\"https://www.healthaffairs.org/doi/10.1377/hlthaff.2018.05493\"\u003eenrolled\u003c/a\u003e in some form of government assistance; in most states, the share of the workforce living below the poverty line is somewhere between \u003ca href=\"https://cscce.berkeley.edu/workforce-index-2020/the-early-educator-workforce/early-educator-pay-economic-insecurity-across-the-states/\"\u003e15 and 25 percent\u003c/a\u003e. An \u003ca href=\"https://www.healthaffairs.org/doi/10.1377/hlthaff.2018.05493\"\u003eongoing study\u003c/a\u003e of 49 early childhood education (ECE) centers in Seattle and Austin found that 42 percent of the workforce was food-insecure. Like other fields with low pay and the subsequent stress and anxiety that accompany poverty, workers have higher rates of chronic disease and are two to five times more likely to report clinically depressive symptoms."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"Child care workers are paid so little, yet the cost of care continues to rise. \u0026ldquo;Child care is my second mortgage,\u0026rdquo; one friend, who\u0026rsquo;s solidly middle-class enough to have a first mortgage, told me. \u0026ldquo;I\u0026rsquo;m lucky that I don\u0026rsquo;t have student loans, because there\u0026rsquo;d be no way for me to pay those plus what I\u0026rsquo;m paying for two kids,\u0026rdquo; said another. Parents love their children\u0026rsquo;s teachers and caregivers, but when confronted with the reality of their low pay, they also wonder: Where is all the money going?"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CorePullquoteBlockType","__typename":"CorePullquoteBlockType","contents":{"html":"Early childhood care is a total market failure, and has been, whether we realized it or not, for decades"},"citation":{"html":""},"placement":"left"},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"These are the hallmarks of a broken system. The cost of care places an unsustainable burden on parents, working to hollow out the middle class and close it off to lower- or working-class parents, but the wages keep many practitioners teetering on the edge of poverty, prompting many of those most adept at the work to seek employment elsewhere. Early childhood care is, as one policy expert put it to me, a total market failure, and has been, whether we realized it or not, for decades."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"How do you fix a fundamentally broken system? It\u0026rsquo;s not as simple as blowing it up and starting over. The failure is so textured, so tied up in ideas of gender and race, of women and work, of \u0026ldquo;choice\u0026rdquo; and \u0026ldquo;kids are best cared for at home,\u0026rdquo; solutions thus far have largely been piecemeal: Add an incentive here, cut a cost there, even take the big step of establishing universal pre-K. Some of these reforms have meaningfully changed kids\u0026rsquo; (and parents\u0026rsquo;) lives, but the entire process feels, as Lea Austin, executive director of UC Berkeley\u0026rsquo;s \u003ca href=\"https://cscce.berkeley.edu/\"\u003eCenter for the Study of Child Care Employment\u003c/a\u003e, put it to me, \u0026ldquo;like pouring water into a bucket with a hole in the bottom.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"The first step toward lasting reform has to be a conceptual one, the sort that will have cascading effects on the entire sector and our society as a whole. We can start thinking of early child care and education the same way we think about public parks or sanitation or libraries or public schools: as a public good, foundational to a functioning society regardless of whether you directly benefit from its existence. Like other public goods, access shouldn\u0026rsquo;t be limited by employment, income, or location, and those who make it run should, at the very least, be paid a living wage."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"This scenario is only possible, of course, when and if early childhood care is robustly funded with tax dollars, just as public schools are. To be clear, tax dollars are already being used to inadequately prop up the system, whether in the form of vouchers for parents, pay supplements for teachers, incentives for practitioners to pursue ECE degrees, or government assistance programs such as Medicare and SNAP for child care workers who cannot pay their bills or provide for their families with their current wages. What if we stopped using tax dollars and massive additional portions of our income to keep pouring water in the bucket, and just plugged the hole?"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"That sounds like a straightforward solution, and in some ways it is. In other ways, it\u0026rsquo;s endlessly complicated. The conversations most people have about child care usually start with the astronomical cost, touch on the difficulty of waiting lists, glance toward the difficulty of finding a \u0026ldquo;good fit\u0026rdquo; (often code for perceived quality), and then stop. Families somehow figure out a way to make it work, even if that means someone dropping out of the workforce, relying on informal \u0026ldquo;kith and kin\u0026rdquo; care, or emptying out savings, and then everyone breathes a deep sigh of relief when the youngest kid hits kindergarten. The struggle to pay for care is acute, but ultimately too short-lived to accumulate political might."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"That can change. Regardless of whether you have children, whether you have three in care or yours have already left the home, we can identify the burden \u0026mdash; and the way it exacerbates racial inequities, sustains the gender pay gap, discourages parenthood, and generally makes life really, really hard for millions of people \u0026mdash; and agree it doesn\u0026rsquo;t have to be this way."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreSeparatorBlockType","__typename":"CoreSeparatorBlockType","blockName":"coreSeparator","placement":"default"},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"In the United States, child care (like \u003ca href=\"https://www.vox.com/the-goods/22297809/student-loan-debt-cancel-forgiveness-middle-class\"\u003epaying for higher education\u003c/a\u003e) is conceived of as an individual problem, with individual solutions. There is an alternate reality for the United States, a clear fork in the road we didn\u0026rsquo;t take. Back in 1971, the Comprehensive Child Development Act made its way through Congress with overwhelming bipartisan support. Building on the success of Head Start, which had launched in 1965, the Child Development Act would\u0026rsquo;ve made high-quality child care available for all on an affordable, sliding scale basis. Nixon vetoed the bill \u0026mdash; a move that, as Anna K. Danziger Halperin, a historian of the child care movement, \u003ca href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/outlook/2020/08/06/richard-nixon-bears-responsibility-pandemics-child-care-crisis/\"\u003eexplains\u003c/a\u003e, \u0026ldquo;surprised even officials within his own administration.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"The reasoning, according to Halperin, was fear from the right wing of the Republican Party that the bill would encourage women to join the workforce, thereby destroying the \u0026ldquo;integrity\u0026rdquo; of the middle-class family unit, while also providing solutions that felt dangerously close to communistic, \u0026ldquo;un-American\u0026rdquo; care. There was also conservative concern that the bill was an \u0026ldquo;overreach\u0026rdquo; into the lives of low-income people, and that its primary beneficiaries were families of color. As Elizabeth Palley, a professor who studies the history of child care, \u003ca href=\"https://www.vox.com/the-highlight/21432940/child-care-bailout-covid-economy-work-parents-great-rebuild\"\u003etold\u003c/a\u003e Vox\u0026rsquo;s Anna North, \u0026ldquo;White people don\u0026rsquo;t want to pay for Black people\u0026rsquo;s children to be cared for.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreImageBlockType","__typename":"CoreImageBlockType","alt":"A child care worker’s hand holds a book while reading to three young girls","hideCredit":false,"placement":"default","credit":{"html":"Illustration by \u003ca href=\"https://carmellekendall.com/\"\u003eCarmelle Kendall\u003c/a\u003e","plaintext":"Illustration by Carmelle Kendall"},"caption":{"html":"","plaintext":""},"contentWarning":{"plaintext":""},"thumbnail":{"size":"original","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/22414716/Spot1_v2.png?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=0,0,100,100","normalizedRatio":0.986351,"originalHeight":1392,"originalWidth":1373,"cropCoordinates":{"height":100,"width":100}},"image":{"id":"dngyOmltYWdlOjMyMjE0","alt":"A child care worker’s hand holds a book while reading to three young girls"}},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"Decades later, patriarchal fear is still alive and well, as evidenced by the state of Idaho\u0026rsquo;s rejection of $6 million in early education funding in March. \u0026ldquo;I don\u0026rsquo;t think anybody does a better job than mothers in the home, and any bill that makes it easier or more convenient for mothers to come out of the home and let others raise their child, I don\u0026rsquo;t think that\u0026rsquo;s a good direction for us to be going,\u0026rdquo; state Rep. Charlie Shepherd \u003ca href=\"https://www.ktvb.com/article/news/local/208/idaho-republican-votes-against-education-funds-convenient-for-mothers-to-come-out-of-the-home/277-645ae7a7-601e-4557-9d7c-f8df5c22949c\"\u003esaid\u003c/a\u003e during the discussion of the bill. \u0026ldquo;We are really hurting the family unit in the process.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"It\u0026rsquo;s too simple to just blame a bunch of right-wing conservative men for where we\u0026rsquo;ve found ourselves; the idea of child care as an individual responsibility was, however inadvertently, supported by feminists as well. After Nixon vetoed the Child Development Act, the broad coalition that had worked to get it through Congress dispersed, including various feminist groups whose focus shifted to getting more women into jobs and combating the gender discrimination that faced them once there."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"\u0026ldquo;They stopped fighting for child care, and other sorts of collective issues, and really focused on individual professional success,\u0026rdquo; Halperin tells me. \u0026ldquo;When you think of child care as a personal choice, it creates all of these new inequalities. Women of color, Black and immigrant women \u0026mdash; they end up caring for the children of professional women. Those professional women, in turn, don\u0026rsquo;t want their whole paycheck going to child care, so it gets undervalued and underpaid.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"This attitude has long been part of the critique of second-wave feminism. In its focus on getting women into the workforce, the movement lost sight of the inequities that were reproduced and exacerbated in the process. Indeed, many white, educated women were satisfied with the child care situation as it was, even with low pay levels for workers, because it still fundamentally worked for \u003cem\u003ethem\u003c/em\u003e. As more and more of those women entered the workforce, regulations that govern the ratio between children and caregivers expanded, and insurance, real estate, and staff turnover costs continued to rise."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"Finding affordable, reliable care has never not been a problem for lower-income women, particularly for women of color. But as early as 1979, Columbia professor Jane Price, author of \u003cem\u003eHow to Have a Child and Keep Your Job\u003c/em\u003e, was referring to the difficulty of finding care, particularly in the suburbs, as \u0026ldquo;\u003ca href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/1979/12/09/archives/westchester-weekly-croton-author-sees-a-childcare-crisis.html?searchResultPosition=1\"\u003ea crisis\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026rdquo; Price predicted that the problem, if ignored, would only get worse, and she was right. Between 1970 and 2000, the cost of care per child rose \u003ca href=\"https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/1-Investing-in-Children-%3A-Changes-in-Parental-on-%2C-Furstenberg/89d01df48596e4d3e43f4dbaf05a138cafb90dfe?p2df\"\u003e200 percent\u003c/a\u003e; today, the average cost of care for families with children under 5 hovers around \u003ca href=\"https://www.americanprogress.org/issues/early-childhood/reports/2019/06/20/471141/working-families-spending-big-money-child-care/#:~:text=The%20results%20of%20the%20child,and%20Human%20Services’%20definition%20of\"\u003e10 percent of a family\u0026rsquo;s monthly income\u003c/a\u003e, with many families paying \u003ca href=\"http://www.freddiemac.com/research/insight/20200107_family_budget_burdens.page\"\u003econsiderably more\u003c/a\u003e."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"The pandemic has clarified \u003ca href=\"https://www.vox.com/2021/3/25/22345214/child-care-covid-19-daycare-american-rescue-plan\"\u003ejust how dire the situation has become\u003c/a\u003e, and there are hopeful steps forward in raising pay and creating universal or sliding scale programs in pockets all over the United States. President Joe Biden\u0026rsquo;s stimulus plan includes \u003ca href=\"https://www.vox.com/2021/1/20/22238757/joe-biden-covid-19-stimulus-child-care\"\u003emore than $25 billion to support child care centers\u003c/a\u003e and $15 million in child care assistance to families, as well as a $3,600 annual tax credit for every child under 6 (and $3,000 a year for children between 6 and 17). If you have children, this shift will directly benefit them \u0026mdash; but it will also benefit you, as their parent, and whoever else\u0026rsquo;s mental and financial load is lessened as a consequence. It will benefit employers, who, as the pandemic has shown, rely on their employees having access to reliable care. It will benefit their coworkers without children. Even if you don\u0026rsquo;t have children, you can see the benefits of a public school system. H 171 promises to fundamentally remake the child care system in the state: No family would be asked to pay more than 10 percent of their income for care, hundreds would be able to stay in their profession, and it would significantly decrease the 10.9 percent of Vermont care workers who, as of 2020, lived in poverty. Decrease the stressors in workers\u0026rsquo; lives \u0026mdash; their need to worry about groceries or their own children\u0026rsquo;s care or health care \u0026mdash; and they become better workers. This is particularly important for child care and child education, which calls workers to be fully engaged and attentive. \u0026ldquo;It seems obvious,\u0026rdquo; Austin says, \u0026ldquo;that we would want anyone working with children to be healthy and supported and as present with children as they possibly can be. They\u0026rsquo;re entrepreneurs and small-business owners, who, as any politician will tell you, form the backbone of the American middle class. When a type of labor is not considered a skill or is conceived of as something that people \u0026ldquo;naturally\u0026rdquo; want to do for free, it keeps wages down. When wages are low, employers have to lower their necessary qualifications, which in turn reinforces the idea that the work is unskilled. In most child care centers in the United States, teaching assistant positions require only a high school diploma or GED, plus a background check. Teacher positions require an associate or bachelor\u0026rsquo;s degree in ECE, but the pay bump is so small that many who obtain their certification, which extends to children in third grade, move to the K-8 system. Various government programs have attempted to \u0026ldquo;upskill\u0026rdquo; the profession, encouraging existing care workers to pursue certification, but the low pay remains a consistent barrier."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"\u0026ldquo;I\u0026rsquo;ve seen providers have to close their doors because they cannot find or pay an assistant,\u0026rdquo; Algorri says. \u0026ldquo;I\u0026rsquo;ve been lucky, but I\u0026rsquo;ve sacrificed a lot to keep the quality of assistants over the years. In \u003ca href=\"https://bookshop.org/books/crawling-behind-america-s-child-care-crisis-and-how-to-fix-it/9781684334278\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eCrawling Behind: America\u0026rsquo;s Childcare Crisis and How to Fix It\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, Elliot Haspel points out that as many as 95 percent of Americans live in child care deserts, where three or more children are in need of care for every available spot; people with above-average income are as likely to live in these areas as people with less income. The number of centers and homes providing care simply cannot keep pace with the demand. Before the pandemic, centers, some of them the only infant-to-3-year-old providers in the area, were closing at alarming rates. As of March 2021, an estimated 20,000 care centers have \u003ca href=\"https://www.thelily.com/20000-day-cares-may-have-closed-in-the-pandemic-what-happens-when-parents-go-back-to-work/\"\u003eclosed permanently\u003c/a\u003e."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"In its current figuration, child care is not a lucrative business. Unlike so many other industries, demand is simply not enough to spark the creation of new businesses. That\u0026rsquo;s how broken the market is, and without a significant refiguration of funding sources, it\u0026rsquo;s not going to change. Her salary is currently just over $28,000, and even though she splits rent for a small home with her partner who works at a nonprofit, they still struggle to cover costs from month to month and live paycheck to paycheck. Her student loans have been in forbearance since she graduated from college in 2019. She recently watched a coworker leave her position to work at a bank, where she now makes double her old salary. Other coworkers regularly leave for fast food jobs."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"At a previous center, the director would often tell workers to be mindful of just how lucky they were, despite their low pay, to have health insurance and paid leave. \u0026ldquo;But these are basic needs of employees,\u0026rdquo; Danielle tells me. \u0026ldquo;Health insurance is a need, not a want. Paid leave is necessary for teachers to still maintain their own lives outside of work.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"\u0026ldquo;It makes me feel so underappreciated that teachers working in K-8 are taken more seriously and paid way more than ECE educators,\u0026rdquo; she continues. \u0026ldquo;But there is a vast difference in how our society views K-8 teachers compared to ECE.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"All over the country, there are people who feel called to the work, who want to continue doing the work, but simply cannot afford to keep doing the work. Because in the end, none of the piecemeal solutions actually address the real problem: that instead of figuring out ways to make care into a public good and caregiving into a good job, many people still think that women, preferably children\u0026rsquo;s own mothers, should be doing this work for free."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"\u0026ldquo;Here in Charleston, there are many [ECE] jobs that start at $9 and $10 an hour,\u0026rdquo; Danielle explains. \u0026ldquo;The profession seems to be looked at as a babysitter, something that a housewife can do for some extra cash,\u0026rdquo; she says. \u0026ldquo;But that\u0026rsquo;s not how we provide quality education and care, and that\u0026rsquo;s not a living wage.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreSeparatorBlockType","__typename":"CoreSeparatorBlockType","blockName":"coreSeparator","placement":"default"},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"Over the past 50 years, the decline of the American middle class has been inseparable from the decline in unionized manufacturing jobs, which paid well enough for many families to be supported by a single salary. As those jobs disappeared, more and more middle-class women entered the workforce, spiking the demand for child care outside the home. The two shifts cannot be untangled: As one sector declined, another rose, at least in part, in response. Both were performed by a mix of workers with and without college degrees, but for many reasons, including a burst of anti-union sentiment and a resistance to viewing child care as a skilled profession, the child care workforce did not unionize en masse. Even as the workforce became more and more essential, the jobs never even came close to providing the sort of pay and benefits that made a manufacturing job a potential pathway to the middle class."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"Those manufacturing jobs aren\u0026rsquo;t coming back, at least not in any approximation of what they once were."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"Instead of waxing about their decline, we could shift our attention to transforming a whole sector of existing jobs, jobs with incredibly high demand, jobs that serve as the backbone of our personal and public lives, into good, middle-class jobs. This transformation could have cascading effects on the lives of the existing, majority-white middle class, many of whose middle-class salaries depend on the ability to access care. The greatest economic effect, however, would be to open up the middle class to over 2 million caregivers \u0026mdash; and remember that \u003ca href=\"https://cscce.berkeley.edu/racial-wage-gaps-in-early-education-employment/\"\u003e40 percent\u003c/a\u003e are women of color."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"UC Berkeley\u0026rsquo;s \u003ca href=\"https://cscce.berkeley.edu/\"\u003eCenter for the Study of Child Care Employment\u003c/a\u003e has calculated what it would cost to transform \u003ca href=\"https://cscce.berkeley.edu/financing-early-educator-quality-a-values-based-budget-for-every-state/\"\u003eeach state\u0026rsquo;s current system\u003c/a\u003e into one that was \u0026ldquo;values\u0026rdquo; based, with a \u0026ldquo;well-qualified and fairly compensated early care workforce\u0026rdquo; providing high standards of care. In Missouri, where Danielle lives, the proposed increase in funding would raise salaries for early educators with bachelor\u0026rsquo;s degrees to $49,626 (on par with elementary and middle school educators) and teacher\u0026rsquo;s assistants to $29,776. Both positions would come with benefits including paid time off and health insurance."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"With this proposal, Danielle\u0026rsquo;s current income would be doubled. \u0026ldquo;It would change my life significantly,\u0026rdquo; she says. \u0026ldquo;It could lead to me living a comfortable, less stressful life. I could pay off my debts, my student loans, and even be able to save for my future.\u0026rdquo; Doubling pay might seem extreme, but these salary increases aren\u0026rsquo;t exorbitant. What they are is stabilizing: for the millions of kids in care, for the millions of parents who depend on it, but also for the millions of women who do this work."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"\u0026ldquo;The stimulus plan is an interesting opportunity to say, we\u0026rsquo;re capable of making significant investments in people when everyone can see the need,\u0026rdquo; Molly Sullivan, the director of national initiatives at First Children\u0026rsquo;s Finance, says. \u0026ldquo;We don\u0026rsquo;t often publicly talk about not being able to afford child care. It\u0026rsquo;s often a private struggle that we keep out of the spotlight, and I think there\u0026rsquo;s an opportunity to see this as a wake-up call. It\u0026rsquo;s okay to acknowledge that what we were doing in the past wasn\u0026rsquo;t working, so let\u0026rsquo;s build a system that didn\u0026rsquo;t exist.\u0026rdquo;"}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"That system will be complicated. You can read about \u003ca href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2019-12-13/how-universal-pre-k-affects-infant-daycare-costs\"\u003ewhat happens\u003c/a\u003e when you extend universal pre-K, as New York, Boston, Nashville, San Antonio, Charlotte, and a handful of other cities have, and don\u0026rsquo;t consider what sucking the older students out of the system does to drive up the price of toddler and infant care. You can deliberate whether the funding should come directly from the federal government or remain the provenance of the states, and how that will reinforce existing discrepancies in educational outcomes in places like Idaho and Mississippi that refuse federal dollars. Some people told me there\u0026rsquo;s too much demand in some areas with universal pre-K, with the same long waiting lists as private preschool, and others directed me to complicated rules about when a pre-K student enters the system, which then make it difficult to give a kid an extra year, if they need it, before moving on to kindergarten."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"It\u0026rsquo;s a tangled set of problems, set against a backdrop of historical and cultural prejudices \u0026mdash; and an undeniable part of the current quagmire, as Anna North \u003ca href=\"https://www.vox.com/22321909/covid-19-pandemic-school-work-parents-remote\"\u003erecently argued\u003c/a\u003e, is the sheer number of hours that Americans have to work to arrive at a living wage. But as Sullivan put it to me, \u0026ldquo;as a country, we have proved that we can tackle big, complicated, incredibly complex things, from the WPA to the Covid vaccine rollout. This is just an area where policy has not really ventured yet.\u0026rdquo; At least not in a substantive way that doesn\u0026rsquo;t just fiddle with the margins \u0026mdash; and at least not since 1971, when Nixon chose that other fork in the road, and mired us in our current, broken approach."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"We\u0026rsquo;ve shown that we can care about inequities even when they don\u0026rsquo;t primarily affect us. We\u0026rsquo;ve shown that we can support public projects even when we are not the primary beneficiaries. If you\u0026rsquo;re drowning in child care costs right now, probably none of this will get done in time to help you. That\u0026rsquo;s the thing about conceiving of child care as a public good: It will serve you, someday, in ways you might not yet appreciate. A public good is good, in some way, for everyone. Plug the hole in the bucket, and the benefits overflow."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":true,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"\u003cem\u003eIf you\u0026rsquo;d like to share your experience as part of the hollow middle class with The Goods, email \u003c/em\u003e\u003ca href=\"mailto:annehelenpetersen@vox.com\"\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eannehelenpetersen@vox.com\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cem\u003e or fill out
